TWO AND A HALF MONTHS LATER



Agatha let herself be dragged out of bed by a very excited, very awake Nicky at six in the morning. He was practically vibrating, a reminder that the slow, forgiving mornings of summer were behind them. She startled like she did every morning, and smacked the top of her alarm clock before remembering that the off button was on the side. She hadn‚Äôt owned a top-snooze alarm since the nineties, but her brain refused the simple adjustment. She had bought the alarm clock in a bout of nostalgia last fall in an attempt to wean herself off late-night scrolling, like all the articles said she should. And now, one year later, she started her mornings with a violent assault of a plastic box, and she still took her phone to bed.

She didn‚Äôt think about it every morning anymore, or every night‚Äîthe fact that her bed was empty.¬†It had been almost two months since Josh had moved out, since their last knock-down, drag-out fight about something so impossibly stupid, they could no longer pretend. Back to School Night

Chapter Summary

in which Agatha still can't catch a fucking break

See the end of the chapter for notes

‚ÄúWe like to think that we are rational beings; humane, conscientious, civilized, thoughtful. But when things fall apart, even just a little, it becomes clear we are not better than animals. Halloween Harvest

"I am too young to die and too old to eat off the kids' menu! What a stupid age I am!"

¬†

On the twenty-fifth day of December, Agatha finally opened her four-day-old mail. She stood in her kitchen, her house too quiet. The front yard was bare of her usual Christmas army‚Äîthree half-made snowmen, two fallen angels, and one shivering, ecstatic little boy.¬†

COMMONWEALTH OF MASSACHUSETTS

CERTIFICATE OF DIVORCE ABSOLUTE

¬†

¬†

So, on the twenty-fifth day of December, between her second and third cup of coffee, a piece of parchment between her forefinger and thumb‚ÄîAgatha‚Äôs heels pressed solidly into the cold kitchen tile.¬†

And if her heels were touching the ground, then this was beat four‚Äîthe certifiable end of the measure. Which meant that, really, the fifth beat was the same thing as the first.

The fifth was the window cracked open to the storm, and fingers cricked tight against her jaw, and glitter under stage lights in June, or December.

Agatha didn‚Äôt rip the certificate in half, or burn it on her doorstep. It would have been too much trouble. The fifth certificate was just another envelope in the mail, stuck between the superstore coupons and the phone plan offerings for the couple next door.¬†

Chapter Summary

well i created a twitter account because i heard y'all were on there... i have learned so much in the last two days lol. @burtletass if you want to say hi

‚ÄúAll freedom is relative‚Äîyou know too well‚Äîand sometimes it‚Äôs no freedom at all, but simply the cage widening far away from you, the bars abstracted with distance but still there, as when they ‚Äúfree‚Äù wild animals into nature preserves only to contain them yet again by larger borders. As a second grade teacher with twenty years of experience and two degrees under her belt, Agatha was more or less an expert on children between the ages of seven and eight. Before she was an expert, when she was just trying to pick a career that didn‚Äôt make her want to walk into traffic, she‚Äôd come across a book at a garage sale: Your Seven Year-old: Life in a Minor Key. Agatha still wasn‚Äôt sure when it was or wasn‚Äôt okay to steal something if you really wanted it. Evanora and Agatha had only moved away for one year before returning to the water-damaged walls of that shitty apartment, and Evanora‚Äôs mental faculties were growing worse by the day. Agatha wasn‚Äôt sure whether it was some sort of medical condition that made her lose her mind and her tongue, or a kind of drug Agatha didn‚Äôt know about, or if it was just the kind of person she was. She read the book on her walk to school her senior year, after she'd decided to stop skipping class. They'd been halfway in before she‚Äôd realized that she was Will Hunting, stuck in Southie with a mother who'd never wanted her and made sure she knew it. The lights in Brandon‚Äôs basement were too bright, and her heartbeat picked up, and she felt that something important was happening.

That something important was definitely not Brandon sticking his tongue down her throat, and she finally shoved him off and snapped, ‚ÄúI‚Äôm trying to watch the fuckin‚Äô movie,‚Äù with the Boston accent that would later fade, and the defiance that would not.

There was her life before that day, and her life after. Walking out of Brandon‚Äôs house as he waved pathetically from the doorstep, she understood what all the teachers had been going on about this whole time: people who went to college got to move away from their mothers.¬†

So she decided on teaching.
